class="IPA" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[baɣˈdaːd]</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Baghdad.ogg" class="internal" title="Baghdad.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>) is the capital of <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>. The population of Baghdad, as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, is approximately 8,765,000,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-population_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-population-2">[note 1]</a></sup> making it the largest city in Iraq, the second largest city in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> (after <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>), and the second largest city in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> (after <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>).</p>/n<p>Located along the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris River</a>, the city was founded in the 8th century and became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>. Within a short time of its inception, Baghdad evolved into a significant cultural, commercial, and intellectual center for the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>. This, in addition to housing several key academic institutions (e.g., <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a>), garnered the city a worldwide reputation as the "Centre of Learning".</p>/n<p>For five centuries from its founding Baghdad was the largest city of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, peaking at a population of more than a million.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> The city was largely <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">destroyed</a> at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> in 1258, resulting in a decline that would linger through many centuries due to frequent plagues and multiple successive empires. With the recognition of Iraq as an independent state (formerly the <a href="/wiki/British_Mandate_of_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="British Mandate of Mesopotamia">British Mandate of Mesopotamia</a>) in 1938, Baghdad gradually regained some of its former prominence as a significant center of <a href="/wiki/Arab_culture" title="Arab culture">Arab culture</a>.</p>/n<p>In contemporary times, the city has often faced severe infrastructural damage, most recently due to the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>, and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> that lasted until December 2011. In recent years, the city has been frequently subjected to <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_insurgency_(Iraq_War)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi insurgency (Iraq War)">insurgency</a> attacks. As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Baghdad was listed as one of the least hospitable places in the world to live,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> and was ranked by <a href="/wiki/Mercer_(consulting_firm)" title="Mercer (consulting firm)">Mercer</a> as the worst of 221 major cities as measured by quality-of-life.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup></p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div class="toctitle">/n<h2>Contents</h2>/n</div>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Surrounding_walls"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Surrounding walls</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a href="#Center_of_learning_.288th_to_13th_centuries.29"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Center of learning (8th to 13th centuries)</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-8"><a href="#End_of_the_Abbasids_in_Baghdad"><span class="tocnumber">2.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">End of the Abbasids in Baghdad</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Ottoman_era_.2816th_to_19th_centuries.29"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Ottoman era (16th to 19th centuries)</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#20th_and_21st_centuries"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">20th and 21st centuries</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Main_sights"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Main sights</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Mutanabbi_Street"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Mutanabbi Street</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Baghdad_Zoo"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Baghdad Zoo</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Mosque_of_the_Kadhimain"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Mosque of the Kadhimain</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Mosque_of_Abu_Hanifah"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Mosque of Abu Hanifah</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Administrative_divisions"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Administrative divisions</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Demographics"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Demographics</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Reconstruction_efforts"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Reconstruction efforts</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Retails"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Retails</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Stadiums"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Stadiums</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Housing"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Housing</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Universities"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Universities</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Institutions"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Institutions</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Major_streets"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Major streets</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Twin_towns.2FSister_cities"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Twin towns/Sister cities</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p>The name Baghdad is pre-Islamic, and its origin is disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup> The site where the city of Baghdad developed has been populated for millennia. By the 8th century AD, several villages had developed there, including a Persian<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> hamlet called <i>Baghdad,</i> the name which would come to be used for the Abbasid metropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-12">[11]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Arab authors, realizing the pre-Islamic origins of Baghdad's name, generally looked for its roots in Persian.<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup> They suggested various meanings, the most common of which was "bestowed by God".<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup> Modern scholars generally tend to favor this etymology,<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup> which views the word as a compound of <i>bagh</i> (<a href="/wiki/File:Baghpahlavi.png" class="image"><img alt="Baghpahlavi.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Baghpahlavi.png/25px-Baghpahlavi.png" width="25" height="22" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Baghpahlavi.png/38px-Baghpahlavi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Baghpahlavi.png/50px-Baghpahlavi.png 2x" data-file-width="189" data-file-height="167" /></a>) "god" and <i>dād</i> (<a href="/wiki/File:Dadpahlavi.png" class="image"><img alt="Dadpahlavi.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dadpahlavi.png/30px-Dadpahlavi.png" width="30" height="26" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dadpahlavi.png/45px-Dadpahlavi.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dadpahlavi.png/60px-Dadpahlavi.png 2x" data-file-width="222" data-file-height="194" /></a>) "given",<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> the first element can be traced to <i>boghu</i> and is related to Slavic <i>bog</i> "god",<sup id="cite_ref-Etymology_Dictionary_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etymology_Dictionary-15">[14]</a></sup> while the second can be traced to <i>dadāti</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Strange_pg_10_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strange_pg_10-16">[15]</a></sup> A similar term in Middle Persian is the name <i>Mithradāt</i> (<i>Mihrdād</i> in New Persian), known in English by its Hellenistic form <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Mithridates (disambiguation)">Mithridates</a>, meaning "gift of <a href="/wiki/Mithra" title="Mithra">Mithra</a>" (<i>dāt</i> is the more archaic form of <i>dād</i>, related to Latin <i>dat</i> and English <i>donor</i><sup id="cite_ref-Etymology_Dictionary_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etymology_Dictionary-15">[14]</a></sup>). There are a number of other locations in the wider region whose names are compounds of the word <i>bagh</i>, including <a href="/wiki/Baghlan" title="Baghlan">Baghlan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bagram" title="Bagram">Bagram</a> in Afghanistan or a village called <a href="/wiki/Baghshan" title="Baghshan">Bagh-šan</a> in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The name of the town <a href="/wiki/Baghdati" title="Baghdati">Baghdati</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> shares the same etymological origins.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup></p>/n<p>A few authors have suggested older origins for the name, in particular the name <i>Bagdadu</i> or <i>Hudadu</i> that existed in Old Babylonian (spelled with a sign that can represent both <i>bag</i> and <i>hu</i>), and the Babylonian Talmudic name of a place called "Baghdatha".<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Some scholars suggested Aramaic derivations.<sup id="cite_ref-EI2_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-9">[8]</a></sup></p>/n<p>When the Abbasid caliph, <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur" title="Al-Mansur">al-Mansur</a>, founded a completely new city for his capital, he chose the name <a href="/wiki/Round_City_of_Baghdad" class="mw-redirect" title="Round City of Baghdad">Madinat al-Salaam</a> or <i>City of Peace</i>. This was the official name on coins, weights, and other official usage, although the common people continued to use the old name.<sup id="cite_ref-.D9.85.D8.A7_.D9.85.D8.B9.D9.86.D9.89_.D8.A7.D8.B3.D9.85_.D9.85.D8.AF.D9.8A.D9.86.D8.A9_.D8.A8.D8.BA.D8.AF.D8.A7.D8.AF_.D9.88.D9.85.D9.86_.D8.B3.D9.85.D8.A7.D9.87_.D8.9F_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-.D9.85.D8.A7_.D9.85.D8.B9.D9.86.D9.89_.D8.A7.D8.B3.D9.85_.D9.85.D8.AF.D9.8A.D9.86.D8.A9_.D8.A8.D8.BA.D8.AF.D8.A7.D8.AF_.D9.88.D9.85.D9.86_.D8.B3.D9.85.D8.A7.D9.87_.D8.9F-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Egabat.google.com_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Egabat.google.com-23">[22]</a></sup> By the 11th century, "Baghdad" became almost the exclusive name for the world-renowned metropolis.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Baghdad" title="History of Baghdad">History of Baghdad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Baghdad" title="Timeline of Baghdad">Timeline of Baghdad</a></div>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation">Foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PARSONS(1808)_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg/220px-PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg/330px-PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg/440px-PARSONS%281808%29_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2380" data-file-height="1864" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PARSONS(1808)_p008_View_of_Bagdad_on_the_Persian_side_of_the_Tigris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nA view of Baghdad from the print collection in Travels in Asia and Africa, etc. (ed. J. P. Berjew, British Library)</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>After the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyads</a>, the first Muslim dynasty, the victorious <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> rulers wanted their own capital from which they could rule. They chose a site north of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid">Sassanid</a> capital of <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> (and also just north of where ancient <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> had once stood), and on 30 July 762<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur" title="Al-Mansur">Al-Mansur</a> commissioned the construction of the city. It was built under the supervision of the <a href="/wiki/Barmakids" title="Barmakids">Barmakids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Mansur believed that Baghdad was the perfect city to be the capital of the Islamic empire under the Abbasids. Mansur loved the site so much he is quoted saying: "This is indeed the city that I am to found, where I am to live, and where my descendants will reign afterward".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The city's growth was helped by its excellent location, based on at least two factors: it had control over strategic and trading routes along the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>, and it had an abundance of water in a dry climate. Water exists on both the north and south ends of the city, allowing all households to have a plentiful supply, which was very uncommon during this time.</p>/n<p>Baghdad eclipsed Ctesiphon, the capital of the Sassanians, which was located some 30&#160;km (19&#160;mi) to the southeast. Today, all that remains of Ctesiphon is the shrine town of <a href="/wiki/Salman_Pak" title="Salman Pak">Salman Pak</a>, just to the south of Greater Baghdad. Ctesiphon itself had replaced and absorbed <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a>, the first capital of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, which had earlier replaced the city of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>.</p>/n<p>In its early years, the city was known as a deliberate reminder of an expression in the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>, when it refers to <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">Paradise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> It took four years to build (764–768). Mansur assembled engineers, surveyors, and art constructionists from around the world to come together and draw up plans for the city. Over 100,000 construction workers came to survey the plans; many were distributed salaries to start the building of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> July was chosen as the starting time because two <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrologers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naubakht" title="Naubakht">Naubakht Ahvazi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mashallah_ibn_Athari" title="Mashallah ibn Athari">Mashallah</a>, believed that the city should be built under the sign of the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_lion" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesopotamian lion">lion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_(astrology)" title="Leo (astrology)">Leo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Leo is associated with fire and symbolises productivity, pride, and expansion.</p>/n<p>The bricks used to make the city were 18 inches (460&#160;mm) on all four sides. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa" title="Abu Hanifa">Abu Hanifah</a> was the counter of the bricks and he developed a canal, which brought water to the work site for the use of both human consumption and the manufacturing of the bricks. Marble was also used to make buildings throughout the city, and marble steps led down to the river's edge.</p>/n<p>The basic framework of the city consists of two large semicircles about 19&#160;km (12&#160;mi) in diameter. The city was designed as a circle about 2&#160;km (1.2&#160;mi) in diameter, leading it to be known as the "Round City". The original design shows a single ring of residential and commercial structures along the inside of the city walls, but the final construction added another ring inside the first.<sup id="cite_ref-ox_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ox-30">[29]</a></sup> Within the city there were many parks, gardens, villas, and promenades.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> In the center of the city lay the <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a>, as well as headquarters for guards. The purpose or use of the remaining space in the center is unknown. The circular design of the city was a direct reflection of the traditional Persian Sasanian <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban design</a>. The Sasanian city of <a href="/wiki/Firuzabad,_Fars" title="Firuzabad, Fars">Gur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fars_Province" title="Fars Province">Fars</a>, built 500 years before Baghdad, is nearly identical in its general circular design, radiating avenues, and the government buildings and temples at the centre of the city. This style of urban planning contrasted with Ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> urban planning, in which cities are designed as squares or rectangles with streets intersecting each other at right angles.</p>/n<div class="thumb tnone" style="clear:both">/n<div class="thumbinner">/n<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow: auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Panoramic view over the ancient city of Babylon, located 85&#160;km (53&#160;mi) south of Baghdad" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg/1074px-The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg" width="1074" height="230" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg/1610px-The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg/2147px-The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8400" data-file-height="1800" /></a></div>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_historical_city_of_Babylon.jpg" title="File:The historical city of Babylon.jpg"></a></div>/n<center>Panoramic view over the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, located 85&#160;km (53&#160;mi) south of Baghdad.</center>/n</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surrounding_walls">Surrounding walls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Surrounding walls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The four surrounding walls of Baghdad were named <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khurasan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>; named because their gates pointed in the directions of these destinations. The distance between these gates was a little less than 2.4&#160;km (1.5&#160;mi). Each gate had double doors that were made of iron; the doors were so heavy it took several men to open and close them. The wall itself was about 44&#160;m thick at the base and about 12&#160;m thick at the top. Also, the wall was 30&#160;m high, which included <a href="/wiki/Merlon" title="Merlon">merlons</a>, a solid part of an embattled parapet usually pierced by <a href="/wiki/Embrasure" title="Embrasure">embrasures</a>. This wall was surrounded by another wall with a thickness of 50&#160;m. The second wall had towers and rounded merlons, which surrounded the towers. This outer wall was protected by a solid <a href="/wiki/Glacis" title="Glacis">glacis</a>, which is made out of bricks and <a href="/wiki/Quicklime" class="mw-redirect" title="Quicklime">quicklime</a>. Beyond the outer wall was a water-filled moat.</p>/n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Gate_Palace">Golden Gate Palace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Golden Gate Palace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>/n<p>The Golden Gate Palace, the residence of the caliph and his family, was in the middle of Baghdad, in the central square. In the central part of the building, there was a green dome that was 39&#160;m high. Surrounding the palace was an <a href="/wiki/Esplanade" title="Esplanade">esplanade</a>, a waterside building, in which only the caliph could come riding on horseback. In addition, the palace was near other mansions and officer's residences. Near the Gate of Syria, a building served as the home for the guards. It was made of brick and marble. The palace governor lived in the latter part of the building and the commander of the guards in the front. In 813, after the death of caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Amin" title="Al-Amin">Al-Amin</a>, the palace was no longer used as the home for the caliph and his family.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> The roundness points to the fact that it was based on <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> The two designers who were hired by <a href="/wiki/Al-Mansur" title="Al-Mansur">Al-Mansur</a> to plan the city's design were <a href="/wiki/Naubakht" title="Naubakht">Naubakht</a>, a Zoroastrian who also determined that the date of the foundation of the city would be astrologically auspicious, and <a href="/wiki/Mashallah_ibn_Athari" title="Mashallah ibn Athari">Mashallah</a>, a Jew from <a href="/wiki/Khorasan_Province" title="Khorasan Province">Khorasan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ref2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref2-34">[33]</a></sup></p>/n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbasids_and_the_round_city">Abbasids and the round city</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Abbasids and the round city">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/220px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/330px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/440px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png 2x" data-file-width="1805" data-file-height="1445" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe <a href="/wiki/Round_city_of_Baghdad" title="Round city of Baghdad">Round city of Baghdad</a> between 767 and 912&#160;AD</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The Abbasid Caliphate was based on their being the descendants of the uncle of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> and being part of the <a href="/wiki/Quraysh_(tribe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quraysh (tribe)">Quraysh</a> tribe. They used <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi'a">Shi'a</a> resentment, <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasanian</a> movement, and appeals to the ambitions and traditions of the newly conquered Persian aristocracy to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Umayyads" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyads">Umayyads</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> The Abbasids sought to combine the hegemony of the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arab</a> tribes with the imperial, court, ceremonial, and administrative structures of the Persians. The Abbasids considered themselves the inherittures and the need of Mansur to place the capital in a place that was representative of Arab-Islamic identity by building the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a>, where ancient texts were translated from their original language, such as Greek, to Arabic. Mansur is credited with the "<a href="/wiki/Translation_Movement" title="Translation Movement">Translation Movement</a>" for this. Further, Baghdad is also near the ancient Sassanid imperial seat of <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> on the Tigris River.</p>/n<h3><span id="Center_of_learning_(8th_to_13th_centuries)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Center_of_learning_.288th_to_13th_centuries.29">Center of learning (8th to 13th centuries)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Center of learning (8th to 13th centuries)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a></div>/n<p>Within a generation of its founding, Baghdad became a hub of <a href="/wiki/Learning" title="Learning">learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a> was an establishment dedicated to the translation of <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> works. Scholars headed to Baghdad from all over the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, facilitating the introduction of Persian, Greek and <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_ancient_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Science and technology in ancient India">Indian science</a> into the Arabic and Islamic world at that time. Baghdad was likely the <a href="/wiki/Historical_urban_community_sizes" title="Historical urban community sizes">largest city in the world</a> from shortly after its foundation until the 930s, when it was tied by <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" class="mw-redirect" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">Córdoba</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Several estimates suggest that the city contained over a million inhabitants at its peak.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Many of the <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i> tales are set in Baghdad during this period.</p>/n<p>Among the notable features of Baghdad during this period were its exceptional libraries. Many of the Abbasid caliphs were patrons of learning and enjoyed collecting both ancient and contemporary literature. Although some of the princes of the previous Umayyad dynasty had begun to gather and translate Greek scientific literature, the Abbasids were the first to foster Greek learning on a large scale. Many of these libraries were private collections intended only for the use of the owners and their immediate friends, but the libraries of the caliphs and other officials soon took on a public or a semi-public character.<sup id="cite_ref-mackensen_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mackensen-38">[37]</a></sup> Four great libraries were established in Baghdad during this period. The earliest was that of the famous Al Mamun, who was caliph from 813 to 833. Another was established by Sabur Ibn Ardashir in 991 or 993 for the literary men and scholars who frequented his academy.<sup id="cite_ref-mackensen_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mackensen-38">[37]</a></sup> Unfortunately, this second library was plundered and burned by the Seljuks only seventy years after it was established. This was a good example of the sort of library built up out of the needs and interests of a literary society.<sup id="cite_ref-mackensen_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mackensen-38">[37]</a></sup> The last two were examples of <i>madrasa</i> or theological college libraries. The Nizamiyah was founded by the Persian Nizam al Mulk, who was vizier of two early Seljuk sultans.<sup id="cite_ref-mackensen_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mackensen-38">[37]</a></sup> It continued to operate even after the coming of the Mongols in 1258. The Mustansiriyah <i>madrasa</i>, which owned an exceedingly rich library, was founded by Al Mustansir, the second last Abbasid caliph, who died in 1242.<sup id="cite_ref-mackensen_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mackensen-38">[37]</a></sup> This would prove to be the last great library built by the caliphs of Baghdad.</p>/n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_Abbasids_in_Baghdad">End of the Abbasids in Baghdad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: End of the Abbasids in Baghdad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg/170px-AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg/255px-AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg/340px-AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1365" data-file-height="2048" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AlKhulafa_Mosque_Iraq.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nAl Khulafa historical Abbasid mosque in Baghdad</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg/170px-Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg/255px-Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg/340px-Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2852" data-file-height="3833" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad-Zumurrud-Khaton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nZumurrud Khaton tomb in Baghdad (built in 1202&#160;AD), photo of 1932</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>By the 10th century, the city's population was between 1.2&#160;million<sup id="cite_ref-Modelski_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modelski-39">[38]</a></sup> and 2&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Baghdad's early meteoric growth eventually slowed due to troubles within the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, including relocations of the capital to <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Samarra" title="Abbasid Samarra">Samarra</a> (during 808–819 and 836–892), the loss of the western and easternmost provinces, and periods of political domination by the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> <a href="/wiki/Buwayhid" class="mw-redirect" title="Buwayhid">Buwayhids</a> (945–1055) and <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" class="mw-redirect" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a> (1055–1135).</p>/n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Seljuks" class="mw-redirect" title="Seljuks">Seljuks</a> were a clan of the <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">Oghuz Turks</a> from the Central Asia that converted to the <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> branch of Islam. In 1040, they destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Ghaznavids" title="Ghaznavids">Ghaznavids</a>, taking over their land and in 1055, <a href="/wiki/Tughril_Beg" class="mw-redirect" title="Tughril Beg">Tughril Beg</a>, the leader of the Seljuks, took over Baghdad. The Seljuks expelled the <a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">Buyid dynasty</a> of Shiites that ruled for some time and took over power and control of Baghdad. They ruled as <a href="/wiki/Sultans" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultans">Sultans</a> in the name of the Abbasid caliphs (they saw themselves as being part of the Abbasid regime). Tughril Beg saw himself as the protector of the Abbasid Caliphs.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Wars in which Baghdad was involved are listed below:</p>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(812%E2%80%93813)" title="Siege of Baghdad (812–813)">Siege of Baghdad (812–813)</a>, Fourth Fitna (Islamic Civil War)</li>/n<li>Siege of Baghdad (865), <a href="/wiki/Caliphal_Civil_War_(865%E2%80%93866)" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliphal Civil War (865–866)">Caliphal Civil War (865–866)</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baghdad_(946)" title="Battle of Baghdad (946)">Battle of Baghdad (946)</a>, Buyid–Hamdanid War</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1157)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1157)">Siege of Baghdad (1157)</a>, Abbasid–Seljuq Wars</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">Siege of Baghdad (1258)</a>, Mongol conquest of Baghdad</li>/n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siege_of_Baghdad_(1401)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Siege of Baghdad (1401) (page does not exist)">Siege of Baghdad (1401)</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Tamerlane</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Baghdad_(1534)" title="Capture of Baghdad (1534)">Capture of Baghdad (1534)</a>, Ottoman–Safavid Wars</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Baghdad_(1623)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capture of Baghdad (1623)">Capture of Baghdad (1623)</a>, Ottoman–Safavid Wars</li>/n<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Siege_of_Baghdad_(1625)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Siege of Baghdad (1625) (page does not exist)">Siege of Baghdad (1625)</a>, Ottoman–Safavid Wars</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Baghdad_(1638)" title="Capture of Baghdad (1638)">Capture of Baghdad (1638)</a>, Ottoman–Safavid Wars</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Baghdad_(1917)" title="Fall of Baghdad (1917)">Fall of Baghdad (1917)</a>, World War I</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/1941_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1941 Iraqi coup d'état">1941 Iraqi coup d'état</a>, World War II</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baghdad_(2003)" title="Battle of Baghdad (2003)">Battle of Baghdad (2003)</a>, United States invasion of Iraq</li>/n</ul>/n<p>In 1058, Baghdad was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Fatimids" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatimids">Fatimids</a> under the Turkish general <a href="/w/index.php?title=Abu%27l-%E1%B8%A4%C4%81rith_Arsl%C4%81n_al-Basasiri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Abu'l-Ḥārith Arslān al-Basasiri (page does not exist)">Abu'l-Ḥārith Arslān al-Basasiri</a>, an adherent of the <a href="/wiki/Ismailis" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismailis">Ismailis</a> along with the 'Uqaylid Quraysh.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Not long before the arrival of the Saljuqs in Baghdad, al-Basasiri petitioned to the Fatimid Imam-Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mustansir_Billah" title="Al-Mustansir Billah">al-Mustansir</a> to support him in conquering Baghdad on the Ismaili Imam's behalf. It has recently come to light that the famed Fatimid <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i" title="Da'i">da'i</a></i>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Mu%27ayyad_al-Shirazi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Al-Mu'ayyad al-Shirazi (page does not exist)">al-Mu'ayyad al-Shirazi</a>, had a direct role in supporting al-Basasiri and helped the general to succeed in taking <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mawṣil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasit,_Iraq" title="Wasit, Iraq">Wāsit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a>. Soon after,<sup id="cite_ref-Daftary.2C_Farhad_1990_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daftary.2C_Farhad_1990-43">[42]</a></sup> by December 1058, a Shi'i <i><a href="/wiki/Adh%C4%81n" class="mw-redirect" title="Adhān">adhān</a></i> (call to prayer) was implemented in Baghdad and a <i><a href="/wiki/Khutbah" title="Khutbah">khutbah</a></i> (sermon) was delivered in the name of the Fatimid Imam-Caliph.<sup id="cite_ref-Daftary.2C_Farhad_1990_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daftary.2C_Farhad_1990-43">[42]</a></sup> Despite his Shi'i inclinations, Al-Basasiri received support from Sunnis and Shi'is alike, for whom opposition to the Saljuq power was a common factor.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tleft">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4429" data-file-height="2786" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nConquest of Baghdad by the Mongols in 1258</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>On 10 February 1258, Baghdad was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Hulegu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hulegu">Hulegu</a>, a grandson of Chingiz Khan (<a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>), during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">siege of Baghdad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Many quarters were ruined by fire, siege, or looting. The Mongols massacred most of the city's inhabitants, including the caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Musta%27sim" title="Al-Musta'sim">Al-Musta'sim</a>, and destroyed large sections of the city. The <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">dykes</a> forming the city's <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> system were also destroyed. During this time, in Baghdad, Christians and Shia were tolerated, while Sunnis were treated as enemies.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> The sack of Baghdad put an end to the Abbasid Caliphate, a blow from which the Islamic civilization never fully recovered.</p>/n<p>At this point, Baghdad was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, a breakaway state of the Mongol Empire, ruling from Iran. In 1401, Baghdad was again sacked, by the Central Asian Turkic conqueror <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> ("Tamerlane").<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> When his forces took Baghdad, he spared almost no one, and ordered that each of his soldiers bring back two severed human heads.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> It became a provincial capital controlled by the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Jalayirid" class="mw-redirect" title="Jalayirid">Jalayirid</a> (1400–1411), Turkic <a href="/wiki/Kara_Koyunlu" title="Kara Koyunlu">Kara Koyunlu</a> (1411–1469), Turkic <a href="/wiki/White_Sheep_Turkmen" class="mw-redirect" title="White Sheep Turkmen">Ak Koyunlu</a> (1469–1508), and the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Safavid" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid">Safavid</a> (1508–1534) dynasties.</p>/n<h3><span id="Ottoman_era_(16th_to_19th_centuries)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_era_.2816th_to_19th_centuries.29">Ottoman era (16th to 19th centuries)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ottoman era (16th to 19th centuries)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Eyalet" title="Baghdad Eyalet">Baghdad Eyalet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Vilayet" title="Baghdad Vilayet">Baghdad Vilayet</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg/220px-Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg/330px-Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg/440px-Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="798" data-file-height="505" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Market-Place_of_Bagdad.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nSouk in Baghdad, 1876</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In 1534, Baghdad was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a>. Under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, Baghdad continued into a period of decline, partially as a result of the enmity between its rulers and Iranian Safavids, which did not accept the Sunni control of the city. <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1623%E2%80%931639)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–1639)">Between 1623 and 1638</a>, it returned to Iranian rule before falling back into Ottoman hands.</p>/n<p>Baghdad has suffered severely from visitations of the <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> and sometimes two-thirds of its population has been wiped out.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></p>/n<p>For a time, Baghdad had been the largest city in the Middle East. The city saw relative revival in the latter part of the 18th century under a <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Mamluk dynasty of Iraq">Mamluk</a> government. Direct Ottoman rule was reimposed by <a href="/wiki/Ali_R%C4%B1za_Pasha_(governor_of_Baghdad)" title="Ali Rıza Pasha (governor of Baghdad)">Ali Rıza Pasha</a> in 1831. From 1851 to 1852 and from 1861 to 1867, Baghdad was governed, under the Ottoman Empire by <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_Nam%C4%B1k_Pasha" title="Mehmed Namık Pasha">Mehmed Namık Pasha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Nuttall_Encyclopedia" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuttall Encyclopedia">Nuttall Encyclopedia</a> reports the 1907 population of Baghdad as 185,000.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_and_21st_centuries">20th and 21st centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 20th and 21st centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Iraq" title="Mandatory Iraq">Mandatory Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg/220px-Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg/330px-Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="599" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shahbandar_Cafe.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe Shabandar Café in Baghdad, 1923</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Baghdad and southern Iraq remained under Ottoman rule until 1917, when captured by the British during World War I. In 1920, Baghdad became the capital of the <a href="/wiki/British_Mandate_of_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="British Mandate of Mesopotamia">British Mandate of Mesopotamia</a> and after receiving independence in 1932, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a>. The city's population grew from an estimated 145,000 in 1900 to 580,000 in 1950. During the Mandate, Baghdad's substantial <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Iraq" title="History of the Jews in Iraq">Jewish community</a> comprised a quarter of the city's population.<sup id="cite_ref-GhareebDougherty2004_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GhareebDougherty2004-52">[51]</a></sup></p>/n<p>On 1 April 1941, members of the "Golden Square" and <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashid Ali">Rashid Ali</a> staged <a href="/wiki/1941_Iraqi_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1941 Iraqi coup d'état">a coup in Baghdad</a>. Rashid Ali installed a pro-<a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">German</a> and pro-<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italian</a> government to replace the pro-British government of <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">Regent</a> <a href="/wiki/%27Abd_al-Ilah" title="'Abd al-Ilah">Abdul Ilah</a>. On 31 May, after the resulting <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iraqi_War" title="Anglo-Iraqi War">Anglo-Iraqi War</a> and after Rashid Ali and his government had fled, the Mayor of Baghdad surrendered to British and Commonwealth forces.</p>/n<p>On 14 July 1958, members of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">Abd al-Karim Qasim</a>, <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">staged a coup to topple the Kingdom of Iraq</a>. King <a href="/wiki/Faisal_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Faisal II">Faisal II</a>, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nuri_as-Said" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuri as-Said">Nuri as-Said</a>, former Regent Prince <a href="/wiki/%27Abd_al-Ilah" title="'Abd al-Ilah">'Abd al-Ilah</a>, members of the royal family, and others were brutally killed during the coup. Many of the victim's bodies were then dragged through the streets of Baghdad.</p>/n<div class="thumb tleft">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg/220px-20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg/330px-20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg/440px-20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5760" data-file-height="3840" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20160102-Tahrir_square_Baghdad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nTahrir square in Downtown Baghdad</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>During the 1970s, Baghdad experienced a period of prosperity and growth because of a sharp increase in the <a href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" class="mw-redirect" title="Price of petroleum">price of petroleum</a>, Iraq's main export. New infrastructure including modern sewerage, water, and highway facilities were built during this period. The masterplans of the city (1967, 1973) were delivered by the Polish planning office Miastoprojekt-Kraków, mediated by Polservice.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a> of the 1980s was a difficult time for the city, as money was diverted by <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> to the army and thousands of residents were killed. Iran launched a number of missile attacks against Baghdad in retaliation for Saddam Hussein's continuous bombardments of Tehran's residential districts.</p>/n<p>In 1991 and 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a> caused <a href="/wiki/Damage_to_Baghdad_during_the_Iraq_War" title="Damage to Baghdad during the Iraq War">significant damage</a> to Baghdad's transportation, <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">power</a>, and sanitary infrastructure as the US-led coalition forces launched massive aerial assaults in the city in the two wars. Also in 2003, the minor riot in the city (which took place on 21 July) caused some disturbance in the population.</p>/n<p>The historic "Assyrian Quarter" of the city, Dora, which boasted a population of 150,000 Assyrians in 2003, made up over 3% of the capital's Assyrian population then. The community has been subject to kidnappings, death threats, vandalism, and house burnings by <a href="/wiki/Alqaida" class="mw-redirect" title="Alqaida">Alqaida</a> and other insurgent groups. As of the end of 2014, only 1,500 Assyrians remained in Dora.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sights">Main sights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Main sights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad(Cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg/220px-Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg" width="220" height="388" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg/330px-Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg/440px-Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad%28Cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="814" data-file-height="1435" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Mutanabbi_Statue_in_Baghdad(Cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/n<a href="/wiki/Al-Mutanabbi" title="Al-Mutanabbi">Al-Mutanabbi</a> Statue at the end of <a href="/wiki/Mutanabbi_Street" title="Mutanabbi Street">Mutanabbi Street</a> beside the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Points of interest include the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Iraq" title="National Museum of Iraq">National Museum of Iraq</a> whose priceless collection of artifacts was looted during the 2003 invasion, and the iconic Hands of Victory arches. Multiple Iraqi parties are in discussions as to whether the arches should remain as historical monuments or be dismantled. Thousands of ancient manuscripts in the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_National_Library_and_Archive" title="Iraq National Library and Archive">National Library</a> were destroyed under <a href="/wiki/Saddam" class="mw-redirect" title="Saddam">Saddam</a>'s command.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutanabbi_Street">Mutanabbi Street</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mutanabbi Street">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mutanabbi_Street" title="Mutanabbi Street">Mutanabbi Street</a></div>/n<p>Mutanabbi Street (Arabic: شارع المتنبي) is located near the old quarter of Baghdad; at Al Rasheed Street. It is the historic center of Baghdadi book-selling, a street filled with bookstores and outdoor book stalls. It was named after the 10th-century classical Iraqi poet <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutanabbi" title="Al-Mutanabbi">Al-Mutanabbi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes2008_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes2008-55">[54]</a></sup> This street is well established for bookselling and has often been referred to as the heart and soul of the Baghdad literacy and intellectual community.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baghdad_Zoo">Baghdad Zoo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Baghdad Zoo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Zoo" title="Baghdad Zoo">Baghdad Zoo</a></div>/n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zoological_park" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoological park">zoological park</a> used to be the largest in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. Within eight days following the 2003 invasion, however, only 35 of the 650 animals in the facility survived. This was a result of theft of some animals for human food, and starvation of caged animals that had no food. <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Anthony" title="Lawrence Anthony">Lawrence Anthony</a> and some of the zoo keepers cared for the animals and fed the carnivores with <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a> they had bought locally.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_radio_4.2C_zoo_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_radio_4.2C_zoo-56">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> Eventually, <a href="/wiki/L._Paul_Bremer" class="mw-redirect" title="L. Paul Bremer">L. Paul Bremer</a>, Director of the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority" title="Coalition Provisional Authority">Coalition Provisional Authority</a> in Iraq from 11 May 2003 to 28 June 2004 ordered protection of the zoo and U.S. engineers helped to reopen the facility.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_radio_4.2C_zoo_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_radio_4.2C_zoo-56">[55]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosque_of_the_Kadhimain">Mosque of the Kadhimain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Mosque of the Kadhimain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Al-Kadhimiya_Mosque" title="Al-Kadhimiya Mosque">Al-Kadhimiya Mosque</a></div>/n<p>Al-Kadhimiyyah Mosque is a shrine that is located in the <a href="/wiki/Kadhimiyyah" class="mw-redirect" title="Kadhimiyyah">Kādhimayn suburb</a> of Baghdad. It contains the tombs of the seventh and ninth <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Shi'ite Imams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Musa_al-Kadhim" title="Musa al-Kadhim">Musa al-Kadhim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_at-Taqi" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhammad at-Taqi">Muhammad at-Taqi</a> respectively, upon whom the title of <i>Kāẓimayn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">كَـاظِـمَـيـن</span>‎‎, "Two who swallow their anger") was bestowed.<sup id="cite_ref-KazemOmmolKitab_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KazemOmmolKitab-58">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dome_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dome-59">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Al-Jawadain2009_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al-Jawadain2009-60">[59]</a></sup> Many Shi'ites travel to the mosque from far away places to commemorate.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosque_of_Abu_Hanifah">Mosque of Abu Hanifah</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mosque of Abu Hanifah">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa_Mosque" title="Abu Hanifa Mosque">Abu Hanifa Mosque</a></div>/n<p>Just as Kadhimiyyah is a predominantly Shi'ite neighborhood with a Mosque that is associated with Shi'ite <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imams</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-KazemOmmolKitab_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KazemOmmolKitab-58">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dome_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dome-59">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Al-Jawadain2009_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al-Jawadain2009-60">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/A%27dhamiyyah" class="mw-redirect" title="A'dhamiyyah">A'dhamiyyah</a> is a predominantly Sunni area with a Mosque that is associated with the Sunni Imam Abu Hanifah. The name of <i>Al-A‘ẓamiyyah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الأَعـظَـمِـيَّـة</span>‎‎) is derived from Abu Hanifah's title, <i>al-Imām al-A‘ẓam</i> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الإِمَـام الأَعـظَـم</span>‎‎, the Great Imam).<sup id="cite_ref-al-Aadhamy_-_p.29_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-al-Aadhamy_-_p.29-61">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_of_the_Mosque_of_Abu_Hanifa_and_its_school_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_the_Mosque_of_Abu_Hanifa_and_its_school-62">[61]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad,_Iraq.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG/220px-Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG/330px-Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG/440px-Baghdad%2C_Iraq.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2008" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baghdad,_Iraq.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nOrbital view of Baghdad</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The city is located on a vast plain bisected by the <a href="/wiki/River_Tigris" class="mw-redirect" title="River Tigris">River Tigris</a>. The Tigris splits Baghdad in half, with the eastern half being called "<a href="/wiki/Al-Rusafa,_Iraq" title="Al-Rusafa, Iraq">Risafa</a>" and the Western half known as "<a href="/wiki/Karkh" title="Karkh">Karkh</a>". The land on which the city is built is almost entirely flat and low-lying, being of <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvial</a> origin due to the periodic large floods which have occurred on the river.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Baghdad has a subtropical <a href="/wiki/Desert_climate" title="Desert climate">desert climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>BWh</i>). In the summer from June to August, the average maximum temperature is as high as 44&#160;°C (111&#160;°F) accompanied by blazing sunshine: rainfall has in fact been recorded on fewer than half a dozen occasions at this time of year and has never exceeded 1 millimetre (0.04&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Even at night temperatures in summer are seldom below 24&#160;°C (75&#160;°F). Baghdad's record highest temperature of 124 degrees Fahrenheit (51 degrees Celsius) was reached in July 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> is typically under 50% in summer due to Baghdad's distance from the marshy southern Iraq and the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storms</a> from the deserts to the west are a normal occurrence during the summer.</p>/n<p>Winters boast temperatures typical of <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical climates</a>. From December to February, Baghdad has maximum temperatures averaging 15.5 to 18.5&#160;°C (59.9 to 65.3&#160;°F), though highs above 70&#160;°F (21&#160;°C) are not unheard of. The average January low is 3.8&#160;°C (38.8&#160;°F) but lows below freezing occur only a couple of times per year on average.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Annual rainfall, almost entirely confined to the period from November to March, averages around 150&#160;mm (5.91&#160;in), but has been as high as 338&#160;mm (13.31&#160;in) and as low as 37&#160;mm (1.46&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> On 11 January 2008, light snow fell across Baghdad for the first time in memory.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup></p>/n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; color:#000000; border-left-width:medium">3,240.8</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source #1: <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-WMO_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-68">[67]</a></sup></td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center; font-size:95%;">Source #2: Climate &amp; Temperature<sup id="cite_ref-climatetemp_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-climatetemp-69">[68]</a></sup></td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baghdad&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg/250px-BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg/375px-BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg/500px-BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="679" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BagdhadFromTheISS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nBaghdad as seen from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Administratively, Baghdad Governorate is divided into <a href="/wiki/Kaza" title="Kaza">districts</a> which are further divided into <a href="/wiki/Nahiyah" title="Nahiyah">sub-districts</a>. Municipally, the governorate is divided into 9 municipalities, which have responsibility for local issues. Regional services, however, are coordinated and carried out by a mayor who oversees the municipalities. There is no single <a href="/wiki/City_council" title="City council">city council</a> that singularly governs Baghdad at a municipal level. The governorate council is responsible for the governorate-wide policy.</p>/n<p>These official subdivisions of the city served as administrative centres for the delivery of municipal services but until 2003 had no political function. Beginning in April 2003, the U.S. controlled <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Provisional_Authority" title="Coalition Provisional Authority">Coalition Provisional Authority</a> (CPA) began the process of creating new functions for these. The process initially focused on the election of neighbourhood councils in the official neighbourhoods, elected by neighbourhood caucuses.</p>/n<p>The CPA convened a series of meetings in each neighbourhood to explain local government, to describe the caucus election process and to encourage participants to spread the word and bring friends, relatives and neighbours to subsequent meetings. Each neighbourhood process ultimately ended with a final meeting where candidates for the new neighbourhood councils identified themselves and asked their neighbours to vote for them.</p>/n<p>Once all 88 (later increased to 89) neighbourhood councils were in place, each neighbourhood council elected representatives from among their members to serve on one of the city's nine district councils. The number of neighbourhood representatives on a district council is based upon the neighbourhood's population. The next step was to have each of the nine district councils elect representatives from their membership to serve on the 37 member Baghdad City Council. This three tier system of local government connected the people of Baghdad to the central government through their representatives from the neighbourhood, through the district, and up to the city council.</p>/n<p>The same process was used to provide representative councils for the other communities in Baghdad Province outside of the city itself. There, local councils were elected from 20 neighbourhoods (Nahia) and these councils elected representatives from their members to serve on six district councils (Qada). As within the city, the district councils then elected representatives from among their members to serve on the 35 member Baghdad Regional Council.</p>/n<p>The first step in the establishment of the system of local government for Baghdad Province was the election of the Baghdad Provincial Council. As before, the representatives to the Provincial Council were elected by their peers from the lower councils in numbers proportional to the population of the districts they represent. The 41 member Provincial Council took office in February 2004 and served until national elections held in January 2005, when a new Provincial Council was elected.</p>/n<p>This system of 127 separate councils may seem overly cumbersome; however, Baghdad Province is home to approximately seven million people. At the lowest level, the neighbourhood councils, each council represents an average of 75,000 people.</p>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a Telecommunication Center in downtown Baghdad</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The nine District Advisory Councils (DAC) are as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a Baghdad</a> (Tisaa Nissan) (9 April)<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<p>The nine districts are subdivided into 89 smaller neighborhoods which may make up sectors of any of the districts above. The following is a <i>selection</i> (rather than a complete list) of these neighborhoods:</p>/n<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; href="/wiki/Al-Jadriya" title="Al-Jadriya">Al-Jadriya</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Al-Hebnaa" title="Al-Hebnaa">Al-Hebnaa</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Zayouna" title="Zayouna">Zayouna</a></li>/n<li><a
